language">English</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Religion</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\nTraditionally <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Non-conformist" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-conformist">non-conformists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dissenters" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissenters">dissenters</a> (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England" title="History of the Church of England">History of the Church of England</a>), as well as other <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>; also Roman Catholics (see <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a>); Islam (see <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_England" title="Islam in England">Islam in England</a>); <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and other faiths (see <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_England" title="Religion in England">Religion in England</a>. Almost 25% are non-religious.<sup id="cite_ref-ks2011_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ks2011-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Related ethnic groups</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (January 2018)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li>other <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British people</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Celtic_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic people">Celtic peoples</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> (esp. <a href="/wiki/Frisians" title="Frisians">Frisians</a>)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:left;">\n<hr /><sup><small>a</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/English_American" class="mw-redirect" title="English American">English American</a>, <sup><small>b</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/English_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="English Australian">English Australian</a>, <sup><small>c</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/English_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="English Canadian">English Canadian</a>, <sup><small>d</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_diaspora_in_Africa" title="British diaspora in Africa">British diaspora in Africa</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>English people</b> are a <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic group</a> native to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> who speak the <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_England" title="English language in England">English language</a>.  The English identity is of <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early medieval</a> origin, when they were known in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> as the <i>Angelcynn</i> ("family of the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>").  Their <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonym</a> is derived from the Angles, one of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> who migrated to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> around the 5th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> England is one of the <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a>, and the majority of people living there are <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British citizens</a>.\n</p><p>Historically, the English population is descended from several peoples&#160;&#8211;&#32;  the earlier Celtic <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Britons (or Brythons)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> that settled in Britain following the withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/Romans" class="mw-redirect" title="Romans">Romans</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frisians" title="Frisians">Frisians</a>.  Collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, they founded what was to become England (from the Old English <i>Englaland</i>) along with the later <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Normans</a> and other groups.  In the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Over the years, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_England" title="Culture of England">English customs and identity</a> have become fairly closely aligned with <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">British customs and identity</a> in general.\n</p><p>Today many English people have recent forebears from other parts of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, while some are also descended from more recent immigrants from other European countries and from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>.\n</p><p>The English people are the source of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> system and numerous major sports such as <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>. These and other English cultural characteristics have spread worldwide, in part as a result of the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#English_nationality"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">English nationality</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Relationship_to_Britishness"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Relationship to Britishness</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Historical_origins_and_identity"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Historical origins and identity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#History_of_English_people"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History of English people</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Early_Middle_Ages"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early Middle Ages</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Vikings_and_the_Danelaw"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Vikings and the Danelaw</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#English_unification"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">English unification</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Norman_and_Angevin_rule"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Norman and Angevin rule</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#In_the_United_Kingdom"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">In the United Kingdom</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Immigration_and_assimilation"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Immigration and assimilation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Current_national_and_political_identity"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Current national and political identity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#English_diaspora"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">English diaspora</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#United_States"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-14"><a href="#Previous_censuses"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Previous censuses</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Canada"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Canada</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Australia"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Australia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Other_communities"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Other communities</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Diaspora"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Diaspora</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notelist"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Notelist</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="English_nationality">English nationality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: English nationality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The concept of an \'English nation\' (as opposed to a British one) has become increasingly popular after the <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> process in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> resulted in the four nations having semi-independent political and legal systems. Although England itself has no devolved government, the 1990s witnessed a rise in English self-consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-KK-262-290_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KK-262-290-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This is linked to the expressions of national self-awareness of the other British nations of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;  which take their most solid form in the new <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> political arrangements within the United Kingdom&#160;&#8211;&#32; and the waning of a shared British national identity with the growing distance between the end of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and the present.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar2003&#91;httpassetscambridgeorg9780521771887sample9780521771887wspdf_1\xe2\x80\x9318&#93;_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar2003[httpassetscambridgeorg9780521771887sample9780521771887wspdf_1\xe2\x80\x9318]-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many recent immigrants to England have assumed a solely British identity, while others have developed dual or mixed identities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECondorGibsonAbell2006_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECondorGibsonAbell2006-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Use of the word "English" to describe Britons from <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_minorities" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic minorities">ethnic minorities</a> in England is complicated by most non-white people in England identifying as British rather than English. In their 2004 Annual Population Survey, the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> compared the <i>ethnic</i> identities of British people with their perceived <a href="/wiki/National_identity" title="National identity">national identity</a>. They found that while 58% of <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white people</a> in England described their nationality as "English", the vast majority of non-white people called themselves "British".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_Britishness">Relationship to Britishness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Relationship to Britishness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It is unclear how many British people consider themselves English. In the <a href="/wiki/2001_UK_census" class="mw-redirect" title="2001 UK census">2001 UK census</a>, respondents were invited to state their ethnicity, but while there were <a href="/wiki/Tick_(checkmark)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tick (checkmark)">tick boxes</a> for \'<a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish\'</a> and for \'<a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish\'</a>, there were none for \'English\', or \'<a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh\'</a>, who were subsumed into the general heading \'White British\'.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Following complaints about this, the 2011 census was changed to "allow respondents to record their English, Welsh, Scottish, Northern Irish, Irish or other identity."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  Another complication in defining the English is a common tendency for the words "English" and "British" to be used interchangeably, especially overseas. In his study of English identity, <a href="/wiki/Krishan_Kumar_(sociologist)" title="Krishan Kumar (sociologist)">Krishan Kumar</a> describes a common slip of the tongue in which people say "English, I mean British". He notes that this slip is normally made only by the English themselves and by foreigners: "Non-English members of the United Kingdom rarely say \'British\' when they mean \'English\'". Kumar suggests that although this blurring is a sign of England\'s dominant position with the UK, it is also "problematic for the English [...] when it comes to conceiving of their national identity. It tells of the difficulty that most English people have of distinguishing themselves, in a collective way, from the other inhabitants of the British Isles".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar20031\xe2\x80\x932_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar20031\xe2\x80\x932-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1965, the historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> wrote,\n</p>\n<dl><dd>"When the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_History_of_England" title="Oxford History of England">Oxford History of England</a></i> was launched a generation ago, "England" was still an all-embracing word. It meant indiscriminately England and Wales; Great Britain; the United Kingdom; and even the British Empire. Foreigners used it as the name of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Power" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Power">Great Power</a> and indeed continue to do so. <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, by origin a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Canadian">Scotch Canadian</a>, was not ashamed to describe himself as "Prime Minister of England" [...] Now terms have become more rigorous. The use of "England" except for a geographic area brings protests, especially from the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scotch</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>However, although Taylor believed this blurring effect was dying out, in his book <i>The Isles</i> (1999), <a href="/wiki/Norman_Davies" title="Norman Davies">Norman Davies</a> lists numerous examples in history books of "British" still being used to mean "English" and vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2010, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Parris" title="Matthew Parris">Matthew Parris</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>, analysing the use of "English" over "British", argued that English identity, rather than growing, had existed all along but has recently been unmasked from behind a veneer of Britishness.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_origins_and_identity">Historical origins and identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Historical origins and identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain</a></div>\n<p>There is a debate between historians, <a href="/wiki/Geneticist" title="Geneticist">geneticists</a> and others about the extent to which historical changes in the culture of the British Isles corresponds to historical migration events of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a>, and to the extent of these migrations. The traditional view of historians, based on contemporary accounts and linguistic evidence, was that the English are primarily descended from the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, the term used to describe the various <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes that migrated to the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_occupation_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman occupation of Britain">Roman occupation of Britain</a>, with assimilation of later migrants such as the Norse <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>.\n</p><p>This belief is now regarded by many historians as incorrect, on the basis of more recent <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> and archaeological research. Based on a re-estimation of the number of settlers, some have taken the view that it is highly unlikely that the British Celtic-speaking population was substantially displaced by the Anglo-Saxons and that instead a process of acculturation took place, with an Anglo-Saxon ruling elite imposing their culture on the local populations.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Research into the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">genetic history of the British Isles</a>, conducted by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a> in 2007 appears to support this theory, not showing a clear dividing line between the English and their \'Celtic\' neighbours but a gradual <a href="/wiki/Cline_(population_genetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cline (population genetics)">clinal</a> change from west coast Britain to east coast Britain, originating from upper palaeolithic and Mesolithic era variations in a pre-Indo-European population, which Oppenheimer argues form the basis of the modern population of the British Isles rather than Germanic tribes or Celts.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-opp_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> More recent genetic studies of ancient British DNA have refuted the hypothesis that the Anglo-Saxon invaders formed an elite class largely separate from the indigenous population, finding that samples from culturally Anglo-Saxon graveyards contained individuals who were more Celtic, suggesting a high level of intermingling between the Anglo-Saxons and the native Britons.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2016 study authored by Stephan Schiffels et al. found the Anglo-Saxons to have significantly impacted the genetic composition of the British Isles, so that on average the contemporary East English population derives 38 percent of its ancestry from Anglo-Saxon migrations, with this proportion varying in other parts of Britain that saw less of the migration or the migration of different Germanic tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The theory that the English people are primarily descended from Anglo-Saxons is based largely on the dramatic cultural changes in Britain following their migration. The Celtic language was almost totally displaced by <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and there was a complete shift towards North-West German farming methods and pottery styles.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, held on for several centuries in parts of western England such as <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> and a part of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many historians, while making allowance for the limited survival of the Britons in England, hold to the view that there was significant displacement of the indigenous population after the Germanic migrations.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_English_people">History of English people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History of English people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_England" title="History of England">History of England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">Sub-Roman Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brython" class="mw-redirect" title="Brython">Ancient Britons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romano-British_culture" title="Romano-British culture">Romano-Britons</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg/220px-Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg/330px-Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg/440px-Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="779" data-file-height="979" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hengest_and_Horsa_Verstegan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Arrival of the First Ancestors of Englishmen out of Germany into Britain": a fanciful image of the Anglo-Saxon migration, an event central to the English <a href="/wiki/National_myth" title="National myth">national myth</a>. From <i>A Restitution of Decayed Intelligence</i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Verstegan" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Verstegan">Richard Verstegan</a> (1605)</div></div></div>\n<p>The first people to be called \'English\' were the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, a group of closely related <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes that began migrating to eastern and southern Great Britain, from southern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, in the 5th century AD, after the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">Romans had withdrawn from Britain</a>. The Anglo-Saxons gave their name to England (<i>Engla land</i>, meaning "Land of the Angles") and to the English.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg/220px-Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg/330px-Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg/440px-Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2196" data-file-height="1480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_Burial_Chamber_Replica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of an <a href="/w/index.php?title=Anglo-Saxon_burial_chamber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anglo-Saxon burial chamber (page does not exist)">Anglo-Saxon burial chamber</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo" title="Sutton Hoo">Sutton Hoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Anglo-Saxons arrived in a land that was already populated by people commonly referred to as the \'<a href="/wiki/Romano-British" class="mw-redirect" title="Romano-British">Romano-British</a>\'\xe2\x80\x94the descendants of the native Brythonic-speaking population that lived in the area of Britain under Roman rule during the 1st\xe2\x80\x935th centuries AD. The multi-ethnic nature of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> meant that small numbers of other peoples may have also been present in England before the Anglo-Saxons arrived. There is archaeological evidence, for example, of an early North African presence in a Roman garrison at <a href="/wiki/Aballava" title="Aballava">Aballava</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Burgh-by-Sands" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgh-by-Sands">Burgh-by-Sands</a>, in Cumbria: a 4th-century inscription says that the Roman military unit <i>Numerus Maurorum Aurelianorum</i> ("unit of Aurelian Moors") from Mauretania (Morocco) was stationed there.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Although the Roman Empire incorporated peoples from far and wide, genetic studies suggest the Romans did not significantly mix into the British population.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The exact nature of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">the arrival of the Anglo-Saxons</a> and their relationship with the Romano-British is a matter of debate. Traditionally, it was believed that a mass invasion by various Anglo-Saxon tribes largely displaced the indigenous British population in southern and eastern Great Britain (modern-day England with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>). This was supported by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Gildas" title="Gildas">Gildas</a>, the only contemporary historical account of the period, describing slaughter and starvation of native Britons by invading tribes (<i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon invasion">aduentus Saxonum</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the English language contains no more than a handful of words borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> sources.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, this view has been re-evaluated by some archaeologists and historians since the 1960s; and more recently supported by genetic studies,<sup id="cite_ref-opp_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> which see only minimal evidence for mass displacement. Archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Pryor" title="Francis Pryor">Francis Pryor</a> has stated that he "can\'t see any evidence for <i>bona fide</i> mass migrations after the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the historian Malcolm Todd writes "It is much more likely that a large proportion of the British population remained in place and was progressively dominated by a Germanic aristocracy, in some cases marrying into it and leaving Celtic names in the, admittedly very dubious, early lists of Anglo-Saxon dynasties. But how we identify the surviving Britons in areas of predominantly Anglo-Saxon settlement, either archaeologically or linguistically, is still one of the deepest problems of early English history."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a survey of the genes of British and Irish men, even those British regions that were most genetically similar to (Germanic speaking) continental regions were still more genetically British than continental: "When included in the PC analysis, the Frisians were more \'Continental\' than any of the British samples, although they were somewhat closer to the British ones than the North German/Denmark sample. For example, the part of mainland Britain that has the most Continental input is Central England, but even here the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Modal_Haplotype" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Modal Haplotype">AMH+1</a> frequency, not below 44% (Southwell), is higher than the 35% observed in the Frisians. These results demonstrate that even with the choice of Frisians as a source for the Anglo-Saxons, there is a clear indication of a continuing indigenous component in the English paternal genetic makeup."<sup id="cite_ref-y-chrom_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y-chrom-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, through the investigation of burials using ancient DNA techniques, researchers found evidence of intermarriage in the earliest phase of Anglo-Saxon settlement. By studying rare mutations and employing whole genome sequencing, it was claimed that the continental and insular origins of the ancient remains could be discriminated, and it was calculated that 25\xe2\x80\x9340% of the ancestry of the modern English is attributable to continental \'Anglo-Saxon\' origins.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vikings_and_the_Danelaw">Vikings and the Danelaw</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vikings and the Danelaw">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a></div>\n<p>From about 800 AD waves of <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danish</a> Viking assaults on the coastlines of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> were gradually followed by a succession of Danish settlers in England. At first, the Vikings were very much considered a separate people from the English. This separation was enshrined when <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alfred_and_Guthrum" title="Treaty of Alfred and Guthrum">Treaty of Alfred and Guthrum</a> to establish the Danelaw, a division of England between English and Danish rule, with the Danes occupying northern and eastern England.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, Alfred\'s successors subsequently won military victories against the Danes, incorporating much of the Danelaw into the nascent kingdom of England. Danish invasions continued into the 11th century, and there were both English and Danish kings in the period following the unification of England (for example, <a href="/wiki/Ethelred_the_Unready" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred II</a> (978\xe2\x80\x931013 and 1014\xe2\x80\x931016) was English but <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a> (1016\xe2\x80\x931035) was Danish).\n</p><p>Gradually, the Danes in England came to be seen as \'English\'. They had a noticeable impact on the English language: many English words, such as <i>anger</i>, <i>ball</i>, <i>egg</i>, <i>got</i>, <i>knife</i>, <i>take</i>, and <i>they</i>, are <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Old_Norse_origin" title="List of English words of Old Norse origin">of Old Norse origin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and place names that end in <i>-thwaite</i> and <i>-by</i> are Scandinavian in origin.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_unification">English unification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: English unification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Wedmore" title="Treaty of Wedmore">Treaty of Wedmore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alfred_and_Guthrum" title="Treaty of Alfred and Guthrum">Treaty of Alfred and Guthrum</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg/300px-Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg.png" width="300" height="372" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg/450px-Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg/600px-Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="994" data-file-height="1233" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_peoples_circa_600.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southern Great Britain in AD 600 after the Anglo-Saxon settlement, showing England\'s division into multiple <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdoms</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The English population was not politically unified until the 10th century. Before then, it consisted of a number of <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdoms</a> which gradually coalesced into a <a href="/wiki/Heptarchy" title="Heptarchy">Heptarchy</a> of seven powerful states, the most powerful of which were <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>. The English <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a> began to form when the Anglo-Saxon kingdoms united against Danish Viking invasions, which began around 800 AD. Over the following century and a half England was for the most part a politically unified entity, and remained permanently so after 959.\n</p><p>The nation of England was formed in 937 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan" title="\xc3\x86thelstan">\xc3\x86thelstan</a> of Wessex after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> as Wessex grew from a relatively small kingdom in the South West to become the founder of the Kingdom of the English, incorporating all <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdoms and the Danelaw.<sup id="cite_ref-Rowse_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rowse-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_and_Angevin_rule">Norman and Angevin rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Norman and Angevin rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haroldoath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ef/Haroldoath.jpg/220px-Haroldoath.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/ef/Haroldoath.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haroldoath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/King_Harold_II" class="mw-redirect" title="King Harold II">King Harold II</a> of England (right) at the Norman court, from the <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> during 1066 brought Anglo-Saxon and Danish rule of England to an end, as the new <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> elite almost universally replaced the Anglo-Saxon aristocracy and church leaders. After the conquest, "English" normally included all natives of England, whether they were of Anglo-Saxon, Scandinavian or Celtic ancestry, to distinguish them from the Norman invaders, who were regarded as "Norman" even if born in England, for a generation or two after the Conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The Norman dynasty ruled England for 87 years until the death of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen of England">King Stephen</a> in 1154, when the succession passed to <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a>, <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">House of Plantagenet</a> (based in France), and England became part of the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a> until 1399.\n</p><p>Various contemporary sources suggest that within 50 years of the invasion most of the Normans outside the royal court had switched to English, with <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a> remaining the prestige language of government and law largely out of social inertia. For example, Orderic Vitalis, a historian born in 1075 and the son of a Norman knight, said that he learned French only as a second language. Anglo-Norman continued to be used by the Plantagenet kings until <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a> came to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Over time the English language became more important even in the court, and the Normans were gradually assimilated, until, by the 14th century, both rulers and subjects regarded themselves as English and spoke the English language.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the assimilation of the Normans, the distinction between \'English\' and \'French\' survived in official documents long after it had fallen out of common use, in particular in the legal phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Presentment_of_Englishry" class="mw-redirect" title="Presentment of Englishry">Presentment of Englishry</a></i> (a rule by which a <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred (country subdivision)">hundred</a> had to prove an unidentified murdered body found on their soil to be that of an Englishman, rather than a Norman, if they wanted to avoid a fine). This law was abolished in 1340.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_United_Kingdom">In the United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In the United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_formation_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the formation of the United Kingdom">History of the formation of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div role="img" class="noresize" style="width: 350px; float: right; clear: right; line-height: 1; text-align: center; background-color: #ffffff; position: relative; max-width: 100%; overflow-x: visible;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/350px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png" width="350" height="272" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/525px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg/700px-Flags_of_the_Union_Jack.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="490" /></a>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:8px; top:72.5px"><a href="/wiki/Flag_of_England" title="Flag of England">St George\'s Cross</a><br />(<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>)</div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:165.5px; top:72.5px"><a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">St Andrew\'s Cross</a><br />(<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>)</div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:106px; top:174px"><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:239px; top:174px"><a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Patrick&#39;s Flag">St Patrick\'s Cross</a><br />(<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>)</div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:165.5px; top:272px"><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a></div>\n</div>\n<p>Since the 18th century, England has been one part of a wider political entity covering all or part of the British Isles, which today is called the United Kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> was <a href="/wiki/Annexation" title="Annexation">annexed</a> by England by the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535%E2%80%931542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542</a>, which incorporated Wales into the English state.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> A new British identity was subsequently developed when <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a> became <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I of England</a> as well, and expressed the desire to be known as the monarch of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1707, England formed a union with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> by passing an <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Act of Union</a> in March 1707 that ratified the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> had previously passed its own Act of Union, so the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> was born on 1 May 1707. In 1801, another <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union</a> formed a union between the Kingdom of Great Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>, creating the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>. In 1922, about two-thirds of the Irish population (those who lived in 26 of the 32 counties of Ireland), left the United Kingdom to form the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>. The remainder became the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland, although this name was not introduced until 1927, after some years in which the term "United Kingdom" had been little used.\n</p><p>Throughout the history of the UK, the English have been dominant in population and in political weight. As a consequence, notions of \'Englishness\' and \'Britishness\' are often very similar. At the same time, after the Union of 1707, the English, along with the other peoples of the British Isles, have been encouraged to think of themselves as British rather than to identify themselves with the constituent nations.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration_and_assimilation">Immigration and assimilation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Immigration and assimilation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Historical_immigration_to_Great_Britain" title="Historical immigration to Great Britain">Historical immigration to Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom_(1922-present_day)" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom (1922-present day)">Immigration to the United Kingdom (1922-present day)</a></div>\n<p>England has been the destination of varied numbers of migrants at different periods from the 17th century onwards. While some members of these groups seek to practise a form of pluralism, attempting to maintain a separate ethnic identity, others have <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interracial_marriage" title="Interracial marriage">intermarried</a> with the English. Since <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Resettlement_of_the_Jews_in_England" title="Resettlement of the Jews in England">resettlement of the Jews</a> in 1656, there have been waves of <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> immigration from <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> in the 19th century and from Germany in the 20th.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the French king <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a> declared <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> illegal in 1685 in the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Fontainebleau" title="Edict of Fontainebleau">Edict of Fontainebleau</a>, an estimated 50,000 Protestant <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> fled to England.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Due to sustained and sometimes mass emigration of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>, current estimates indicate that around 6 million people in the UK have at least one grandparent born in the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There has been a <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black</a> presence in England since the 16th century due to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery#Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="History of slavery">slave trade</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-blackhistory_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackhistory-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and an <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian</a> presence since at least the 17th century because of the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-blackhistory_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackhistory-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_British" title="Black British">Black</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">Asian</a> populations have grown throughout the UK generally, as immigration from the British Empire and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> was encouraged due to labour shortages during post-war rebuilding.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> \nHowever, these groups are often still considered to be ethnic minorities and research has shown that black and Asian people in the UK are more likely to identify as British rather than with one of the state\'s four constituent nations, including England.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_national_and_political_identity">Current national and political identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Current national and political identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 1990s witnessed a resurgence of English national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Survey data shows a rise in the number of people in England describing their national identity as English and a fall in the number describing themselves as British.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Today, black and minority ethnic people of England still generally identify as British rather than English to a greater extent than their white counterparts;<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> however, groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_an_English_Parliament" title="Campaign for an English Parliament">Campaign for an English Parliament</a> (CEP) suggest the emergence of a broader civic and multi-ethnic English nationhood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Scholars and journalists have noted a rise in English self-consciousness, with increased use of the <a href="/wiki/English_flag" class="mw-redirect" title="English flag">English flag</a>, particularly at football matches where the <a href="/wiki/Union_flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union flag">Union flag</a> was previously more commonly flown by fans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar2003262_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar2003262-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This perceived rise in English self-consciousness has generally been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> in the late 1990s of some powers to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In policy areas for which the devolved administrations in Scotland, Wales and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> have responsibility, the UK Parliament votes on laws that consequently only apply to England. Because the Westminster Parliament is composed of MPs from throughout the United Kingdom, this has given rise to the "<a href="/wiki/West_Lothian_question" title="West Lothian question">West Lothian question</a>", a reference to the situation in which MPs representing constituencies outside England can vote on matters affecting only England, but MPs cannot vote on the same matters in relation to the other parts of the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, groups such as the CEP have called for the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Devolved_English_parliament" title="Devolved English parliament">devolved English Parliament</a>, claiming that there is now a discriminatory democratic deficit against the English. The establishment of an English parliament has also been backed by a number of Scottish and Welsh nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Writer <a href="/wiki/Paul_Johnson_(writer)" title="Paul Johnson (writer)">Paul Johnson</a> has suggested that like most dominant groups, the English have only demonstrated interest in their ethnic self-definition when they were feeling oppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Curtice" title="John Curtice">John Curtice</a> argues that "In the early years of devolution...there was little sign" of an English backlash against devolution for Scotland and Wales, but that more recently survey data shows tentative signs of "a form of English nationalism...beginning to emerge among the general public".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Michael Kenny, <a href="/wiki/Richard_English" title="Richard English">Richard English</a> and Richard Hayton, meanwhile, argue that the resurgence in English nationalism predates devolution, being observable in the early 1990s, but that this resurgence does not necessarily have negative implications for the perception of the UK as a political union.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Others question whether devolution has led to a rise in English national identity at all, arguing that survey data fails to portray the complex nature of national identities, with many people considering themselves both English <i>and</i> British.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECondorGibsonAbell2006128_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECondorGibsonAbell2006128-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent surveys of public opinion on the establishment of an English parliament have given widely varying conclusions. In the first five years of devolution for Scotland and Wales, support in England for the establishment of an English parliament was low at between 16 and 19%, according to successive <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes_Survey" title="British Social Attitudes Survey">British Social Attitudes Surveys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> A report, also based on the British Social Attitudes Survey, published in December 2010 suggests that only 29% of people in England support the establishment of an English parliament, though this figure had risen from 17% in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\nOne 2007 poll carried out for <a href="/wiki/BBC_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="BBC TV">BBC</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Newsnight" title="Newsnight">Newsnight</a></i>, however, found that 61 per cent would support such a parliament being established.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Krishan Kumar notes that support for measures to ensure that only English MPs can vote on legislation that applies only to England is generally higher than that for the establishment of an English parliament, although support for both varies depending on the timing of the opinion poll and the wording of the question.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar2010484_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar2010484-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Electoral support for English nationalist parties is also low, even though there is public support for many of the policies they espouse.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Democrats" title="English Democrats">English Democrats</a> gained just 64,826 votes in the <a href="/wiki/Results_of_the_United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="Results of the United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 UK general election</a>, accounting for 0.3 per cent of all votes cast in England.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Kumar argued in 2010 that "despite devolution and occasional bursts of English nationalism \xe2\x80\x93 more an expression of exasperation with the Scots or Northern Irish \xe2\x80\x93 the English remain on the whole satisfied with current constitutional arrangements".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKumar2010478_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar2010478-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="English_diaspora">English diaspora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: English diaspora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="float:right;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="5">Number of the English diaspora\n</th></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" class="unsortable">Year<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th scope="col" class="unsortable">Country\n</th>\n<th>Population\n</th>\n<th>% of local population\n</th>\n<th scope="col" class="unsortable"><abbr title="Reference(s)">Ref(s)</abbr>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:30px;"><a href="/wiki/2016_Australian_census" title="2016 Australian census">2016</a></td>\n<td>Australia</td>\n<td>7,852,224</td>\n<td>36.1%</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:30px;"><a href="/wiki/Canada_2016_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2016 Census">2016</a></td>\n<td>Canada</td>\n<td>6,320,085</td>\n<td>18.3%</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-Census_Profile,_2016_Census_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_Profile,_2016_Census-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:30px;"><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011</a></td>\n<td>Scotland</td>\n<td>459,486</td>\n<td>8.68%</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:30px;">2016</td>\n<td>United States<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>23,835,787</td>\n<td>7.4%</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-factfinder.census.gov_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder.census.gov-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:30px;"><a href="/wiki/2013_New_Zealand_census" title="2013 New Zealand census">2013</a></td>\n<td>New Zealand</td>\n<td>38,916<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x93215,589<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>5.41%</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-2013_Census_results:_New_Zealand_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013_Census_results:_New_Zealand-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/English_diaspora" title="English diaspora">English diaspora</a></div>\n<p>From the earliest times English people have left England to settle in other parts of Great Britain and Northern Ireland, but it is not possible to identify their numbers, as British censuses have historically not invited respondents to identify themselves as English.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> However, the census does record place of birth, revealing that 8.08% of Scotland\'s population,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> 3.66% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and 20% of the Welsh population were born in England.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the census of the Republic of Ireland does not collect information on ethnicity, but it does record that there are over 200,000 people living in Ireland who were born in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English ethnic descent and emigrant communities are found primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Western_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Western World">Western World</a>, and in some places, settled in significant numbers. Substantial populations descended from English colonists and immigrants exist in the United States, Canada, Australia, South Africa and New Zealand.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/200px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg" width="200" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/300px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/400px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1588" data-file-height="1932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, known as the "Father of His Country," and first <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, had English ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Americans" title="English Americans">English Americans</a></div>\n<p>In the 2016 <a href="/wiki/American_Community_Survey" title="American Community Survey">American Community Survey</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_American" class="mw-redirect" title="English American">English Americans</a> were (7.4%) of the United States population behind the <a href="/wiki/German_American" class="mw-redirect" title="German American">German Americans</a> (13.9%) and <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish American">Irish Americans</a> (10.0%).<sup id="cite_ref-factfinder.census.gov_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder.census.gov-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demographers</a> regard this as a serious undercount, as the index of inconsistency is high, and many, if not most, people from English stock have a tendency (since the introduction of a new \'American\' category in the 2000 census) to identify as simply <a href="/wiki/American_ethnicity" class="mw-redirect" title="American ethnicity">Americans</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> or if of mixed European ancestry, identify with a more recent and differentiated ethnic group.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_censuses">Previous censuses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Previous censuses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">2000 census</a>, 24,509,692 Americans described their <a href="/wiki/Ancestry" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancestry">ancestry</a> as wholly or partly English. In addition, 1,035,133 recorded British ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This was a numerical decrease from the census in <a href="/wiki/1990_United_States_Census" title="1990 United States Census">1990</a> where 32,651,788 people or 13.1% of the population self-identified with English ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/1980_United_States_Census" title="1980 United States Census">1980 census</a>, over 49 million (49,598,035) Americans claimed English ancestry, at the time around 26.34% of the total population and largest reported group which, even today, would make them the largest ethnic group in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish Americans</a> are descendants of <a href="/wiki/Lowland_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowland Scotland">Lowland Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_England_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern England English">Northern English</a> (specifically: <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">County Durham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Westmorland" title="Westmorland">Westmorland</a>) settlers who colonised Ireland during the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">Plantation of Ulster</a> in the 17th century.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">Americans</a> of English heritage are often seen, and identify, as simply "American" due to the many historic cultural ties between England and the U.S. and their influence on the country\'s population. Relative to ethnic groups of other European origins, this may be due to the early establishment of English settlements; as well as to non-English groups having emigrated in order to establish significant communities.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Canadians" title="English Canadians">English Canadians</a></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Canada_2016_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2016 Census">Canada 2016 Census</a>, \'English\' was the most common ethnic origin (ethnic origin refers to the ethnic or cultural group(s) to which the respondent\'s ancestors belong<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>) recorded by respondents; 6,320,085 people or 18.3% of the population self-identified themselves as wholly or partly English.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_Profile,_2016_Census_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_Profile,_2016_Census-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, people identifying as Canadian but not English may have previously identified as English before the option of identifying as Canadian was available.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EBarton2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/EBarton2.jpg/230px-EBarton2.jpg" width="230" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/EBarton2.jpg/345px-EBarton2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/EBarton2.jpg/460px-EBarton2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="915" data-file-height="661" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EBarton2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edmund_Barton" title="Edmund Barton">Edmund Barton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Deakin" title="Alfred Deakin">Alfred Deakin</a>, 1st and 2nd <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Australia" title="Prime Minister of Australia">Prime Minister of Australia</a> both had English parents.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Australians" title="English Australians">English Australians</a></div>\n<p>From the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia_(1788-1850)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Australia (1788-1850)">colonial era</a> until the mid-20th century, the vast majority of settlers to Australia were from the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, with the English being the dominant group. Among the leading ancestries, increases in Australian, Irish and German ancestries and decreases in English, Scottish and Welsh ancestries appear to reflect such shifts in perception or reporting. These reporting shifts at least partly resulted from changes in the design of the census question, in particular the introduction of a tick box format in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-abs.gov.au_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abs.gov.au-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\nEnglish Australians have more often come from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_England" title="Southern England">south</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">north of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Australians" title="Australians">Australians</a> of English descent, are both the single largest ethnic group in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and the largest \'ancestry\' identity in the Australian census.<sup id="cite_ref-Census2016-036_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census2016-036-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the <a href="/wiki/2016_Australian_census" title="2016 Australian census">2016 census</a>, 7.8 million or 36.1% of the population identified as "English" or a combination including English, a numerical increase from 7.2 million over the 2011 census figure. The census also documented 907,572 residents or 3.9% of Australia as being born in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, and are the largest overseas-born population.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_communities">Other communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since the 1980s there have been increasingly large numbers of English people, estimated at over 3 million, permanently or semi-permanently living in Spain and France, drawn there by the climate and cheaper house prices.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s).
